                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>As the only major crop domesticated in North America, with its sun-like inflorescence that inspired artists, the sunflower is both a social icon and a major research focus for scientists. In evolutionary biology, the <i>Helianthus</i> genus is a long-time model for hybrid speciation and adaptive introgression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Rieseberg, L. H., Van Fossen, C. &amp; Desrochers, A. M. Hybrid speciation accompanied by genomic reorganization in wild sunflowers. Nature 375, 313–316 (1995)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1498">10</a></sup>. In plant science, the sunflower is a model for understanding solar tracking<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Vandenbrink, J. P., Brown, E. A., Harmer, S. L. &amp; Blackman, B. K. Turning heads: the biology of solar tracking in sunflower. Plant Sci. 224, 20–26 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1502">11</a></sup> and inflorescence development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tähtiharju, S. et al. Evolution and diversification of the CYC/TB1 gene family in Asteraceae—a comparative study in Gerbera (Mutisieae) and sunflower (Heliantheae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1155–1166 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1506">12</a></sup>. Despite this large interest, assembling its genome has been extremely difficult as it mainly consists of long and highly similar repeats. This complexity has challenged leading-edge assembly protocols for close to a decade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kane, N. C. et al. Progress towards a reference genome for sunflower. Botany 89, 429–437 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1510">13</a></sup>.</p><p>To finally overcome this challenge, we generated a 102× sequencing coverage of the genome of the inbred line XRQ using 407 single-molecule real-time (SMRT) cells on the PacBio RS II platform. Production of 32 million very long reads allowed us to generate a genome assembly that captures 3 gigabases (Gb) (80% of the estimated genome size) in 13,957 sequence contigs. Four high-density genetic maps were combined with a sequence-based physical map to build the sequences of the 17 pseudo-chromosomes that anchor 97% of the gene content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.1–1.6</a>). This compares favourably to an assembly of another sunflower genotype (HA412-HO; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.7</a>), based on second-generation sequencing data, in which 2 Gb of sequence are placed in 816,854 contigs and 31,392 scaffolds. The sunflower genome encodes 52,232 inferred protein-coding genes and 5,803 spliced long non-coding RNAs (lncRNAs, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.1</a>). To build the first small-RNA-mediated regulatory network for the sunflower, we identified 123 microRNA (miRNA) genes that we classified into 43 families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 1</a>), including 16 novel families. Sixty-three lncRNAs and 1,020 mRNAs are predicted to be miRNA targets, including 71 loci that probably produce secondary phased short-interfering RNAs (siRNAs, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The sunflower genome assembly allows integration of diversity, genetics and expression data."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The sunflower genome assembly allows integration of diversity, genetics and expression data.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22380/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22380/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22380_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22380/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22380_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Circular representation of the pseudomolecules. <b>b</b>, Density of two families of long retrotransposon terminal repeats (5.25–9.5 kb in blue and 9.5–12.25 kb in red) and genes (purple). <b>c</b>, SNP density in 80 lines of the domesticated sunflower. <b>d</b>, Locations of genes mapping to oil metabolic pathways. <b>e</b>, QTLs for seven oil-related traits, whereby the colour (from light to dark) indicates the trait: palmitate, linoleate, oil content, oleate, phytosterol, stearate and tocopherol. <b>f</b>, Regions associated with flowering time in domesticated sunflowers. <b>g</b>, Location of homologues of <i>A. thaliana</i> flowering genes. <b>h</b>, Expression of organ-specific genes (from outside to inside tracks: pollen, stamen, pistil, disc floret ovary, ray floret ovary, disc floret corolla, bract, ray floret ligule, leaf, stem and root).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM10">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22380/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>More than three quarters of the sunflower genome consisted of long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), of which 59% belong to the <i>Gypsy</i> evolutionary lineage. Sunflower LTR-RT lineages are predominantly young and exhibit minimal sequence divergence owing to significant expansion in the past one million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Staton, S. E. et al. The sunflower (Helianthus annuus L.) genome reflects a recent history of biased accumulation of transposable elements. Plant J. 72, 142–153 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1595">5</a></sup>. This pattern contrasts with that of DNA transposons, where the greatest density of insertions is 2–4 million years old (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig3">Extended Data Fig. 1</a>). The LTR-RTs in the sunflower exhibit non-random patterns of chromosomal distribution and are predominantly intact (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig4">Extended Data Fig. 2</a> <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 2.3.1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">2.3.2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.3</a>). We found that LTR sequences display an elevated transition-to-transversion ratio, similar to that of maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Vitte, C. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analysis of retrotransposon structural diversity uncovers properties and propensities in angiosperm genome evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 17638–17643 (2006)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1615">14</a></sup>, probably reflecting the outcomes of epigenetic silencing. We discovered that more than 6,000 transposons have acquired gene fragments, and Helitron transposons contained significantly more gene fragments than other transposon types (<i>P</i> = 2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>). In addition, 8% of Helitrons contained more than one gene fragment, with the most commonly acquired sequences being related to metabolism and defence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2.3.4</a>). These findings highlight the creative potential of transposons and provide tools for understanding gene function in this model system.</p><p>To assess the palaeohistory of the Asterid family, we performed a comparative genomic investigation of the sunflower with lettuce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Truco, M. J. et al. An ultra-high-density, transcript-based, genetic map of lettuce. G3 (Bethesda) 3, 617–631 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1630">15</a></sup> and artichoke<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1634">16</a></sup> as representatives of Asterids II, coffee as a representative of Asterids I (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1637">17</a>) and grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1641">18</a></sup> as an outgroup. The grape genome is considered to be the closest modern representative of the ancestral eudicot karyotype (AEK) consisting of 7 (pre-γ ancestor) or 21 (post-γ ancestor) protochromosomes, with γ indicating the ancestral whole-genome triplication of the Eudicots (WGT-γ)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Salse, J. Ancestors of modern plant crops. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 134–142 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1645">19</a></sup>. We identified orthologous genes between the sunflower and grape–coffee–lettuce–artichoke as well as paralogous genes within the sunflower (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 3.1</a>), coffee and artichoke genomes. In addition to WGT-γ (common with grape, artichoke, lettuce, coffee and sunflower) we established that sunflower, lettuce and artichoke experienced a whole-genome triplication (WGT-1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Truco, M. J. et al. An ultra-high-density, transcript-based, genetic map of lettuce. G3 (Bethesda) 3, 617–631 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1656">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1659">16</a></sup>, which has recently been proposed as independent genome duplications that are close in time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Barker, M. S. et al. Most Compositae (Asteraceae) are descendants of a paleohexaploid and all share a paleotetraploid ancestor with the Calyceraceae. Am. J. Bot. 103, 1203–1211 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1663">6</a></sup>. A minimum of 3 chromosomal fissions and 57 chromosomal fusions were necessary for the lettuce to reach its current structure of 9 chromosomes, and 14 fissions and 60 fusions for the artichoke to reach 17 modern chromosomes. The sunflower experienced a much more complex evolutionary history with a lineage-specific whole-genome duplication (WGD-2, around 29 million years ago), in addition to the shared ancestral WGT-γ (dating back to around 122–164 million years ago) and WGT-1 (around 38–50 million years ago), plus 17 chromosomal fissions and 126 chromosomal fusions that finally shaped the present-day karyotype of 17 chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). The <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) of paralogues clearly illustrates the different rounds (WGD-2, WGT-1 and WGT-γ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Barker, M. S. et al. Multiple paleopolyploidizations during the evolution of the Compositae reveal parallel patterns of duplicate gene retention after millions of years. Mol. Biol. Evol. 25, 2445–2455 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1678">7</a></sup>) of polyploidization events experienced by the sunflower so that for any ancestral region from the <i>n</i> = 7 AEK, a maximum number of 18 inherited regions are currently expected to be found in the modern sunflower genome. The dot plots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>) illustrate the paralogues inherited from WGD-2 in the sunflower genome (2–2 diagonal relationships), the paralogues deriving from WGT-1 in the artichoke genome (3–3 diagonal relationships) and finally the WGT-γ paralogues in the coffee genome (3–3 diagonal relationships). Thus, for any ancestral regions from the AEK (post-γ <i>n</i> = 21) the complete repertoire of 6–3–1–3 orthologous regions in the sunflower–artichoke–coffee–lettuce, respectively, is provided (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Sunflower evolutionary history."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Sunflower evolutionary history.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22380/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22380/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22380_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature22380/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature22380_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="894" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Evolutionary scenario of the Asterids (sunflower, artichoke, lettuce and coffee) from the AEKs of 21 (post-WGT-γ) and 7 (pre-WGT-γ) protochromosomes. The modern genomes are illustrated at the bottom with the different colours reflecting the origin from the seven ancestral chromosomes from the <i>n</i> = 7 AEK (top). Polyploidization events are shown with coloured dots (duplications) and stars (triplications), along with the shuffling events (fusions and fissions). The time scale is shown on the left (million years). <b>b</b>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions. Left <i>y</i> axis, sunflower paralogues (black); right <i>y</i> axis, coffee paralogues (orange), artichoke paralogues (blue) and sunflower–coffee orthologues (purple). Polyploidization (WGT-1, WGD-2 and WGT-γ) and speciation (sunflower–coffee) events are referenced on the <i>x</i> axis. <b>c</b>, Dot plots of paralogues in sunflower, artichoke and coffee genomes illustrating, respectively, WGD-2 (1–2 chromosomal relationships in red circles), WGT-1 (1–3 relationships in blue circles) and WGT-γ (1–3 relationships in brown circles) events.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM11">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature22380/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The evolution of the cultivated sunflower progressed in two steps, domestication by native North Americans, followed by breeding involving selection on traits related to modern agricultural production. We applied an integrative approach to identify candidate genes for two major breeding traits: flowering time and seed oil content and quality. Sunflower gene networks were reconstructed with a supervised orthology-based transfer of knowledge from model species for both traits. Network genes that co-localized with genomic regions associated with variation in the traits of interest were further investigated by exploiting new information on paralogy relationships, expression and diversity data. We generated and integrated 58 transcriptomes for the roots, stem, leaves and eight floral organs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig1">Fig. 1h</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 5, 6</a>), and for the leaves and/or roots following application of nine hormones and three abiotic stress treatments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4.1–4.3</a>). In addition, we re-sequenced 80 domesticated lines (10–20× coverage) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 5.1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">5.2</a>). The integrative web interface Heliagene provides visualization, querying tools for data mining and network exploration for the community (<a href="https://www.heliagene.org">https://www.heliagene.org</a>).</p><p>Reconstructing the flowering-time genetic network in sunflower is of particular interest, because it is a key trait in crop production and the best-adapted flowering time has been selected in each cropping area during the breeding phase. Taking advantage of a recently developed database of flowering-time gene networks in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bouché, F., Lobet, G., Tocquin, P. &amp; Périlleux, C. FLOR-ID: an interactive database of flowering-time gene networks in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nucleic Acids Res. 44 (D1), D1167–D1171 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1784">20</a></sup>, we identified 485 orthologues and in-paralogues (that is, paralogues post-dating speciation) for 270 flowering-time genes in the sunflower genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 6.2</a>). There were several sunflower in-paralogues for 180 <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes, illustrating the complexity of regulatory networks in sunflower.</p><p>Previous investigations of flowering-time architecture in the sunflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Blackman, B. K. et al. Contributions of flowering time genes to sunflower domestication and improvement. Genetics 187, 271–287 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1803">21</a></sup>, using more limited genomic data, focused on the transition from the wild sunflower to early domesticates. Whether flowering-time variation among modern lines involves the same genomic regions and gene families has broad implications for understanding pre- and post-domestication selection. Furthermore, the identification of ohnologous regions (that is, regions originating from whole-genome duplication) in the sunflower genome offers an excellent opportunity to determine the extent of functional diploidization for a quantitative trait in a complex genome. We used genome-wide association studies (GWAS) to dissect the genetic basis of flowering-time variation in a set of 480 <i>F</i><sub>1</sub> hybrids obtained from 72 inbred lines, identifying 35 genomic regions associated with flowering time (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 5a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 6.1</a>). Comparison with flowering-time quantitative trait loci (QTLs) associated with domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Blackman, B. K. et al. Contributions of flowering time genes to sunflower domestication and improvement. Genetics 187, 271–287 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1817">21</a></sup> suggests that similar genomic regions are responsible for variation among modern cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 6.2</a>), possibly because selection during domestication has not been intense enough to eliminate variation at those loci, or because introgressions during sunflower breeding have reintroduced wild alleles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Baute, G. J., Kane, N. C., Grassa, C. J., Lai, Z. &amp; Rieseberg, L. H. Genome scans reveal candidate domestication and improvement genes in cultivated sunflower, as well as post-domestication introgression with wild relatives. New Phytol. 206, 830–838 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1825">22</a></sup>. The genomic architecture of flowering time has been shaped by the most recent whole-genome duplication (WGD-2), with more pairs of duplicated blocks associated with flowering time than is expected by chance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 5b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Tab1">Extended Data Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 7</a>). Therefore, even ancient ohnologues remain involved in the same regulatory networks and complete functional diploidization after whole-genome duplication may take long to achieve. Our integrative approach also highlights new candidate genes such as a newly discovered <i>AGL24</i> in-paralogue, which directly colocalizes with single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) associated with flowering time and new <i>FT</i> paralogues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 5c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 6.2</a>). This analysis therefore provides insights into the architecture of flowering time in domesticated sunflowers and provides a major resource for breeding programs.</p><p>Seed oil content and quality have been under selection during sunflower improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Chapman, M. A. &amp; Burke, J. M. Evidence of selection on fatty acid biosynthetic genes during the evolution of cultivated sunflower. Theor. Appl. Genet. 125, 897–907 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1854">23</a></sup> and continue to be a primary target of breeding programs. To determine the genetic bases of these traits, we reconstructed a genome-scale metabolic network for the sunflower (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 6a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 8.1</a>) and extracted metabolic pathways involved in oil synthesis, yielding a total of 429 genes mapped onto 125 reactions, corresponding to 12 pathways (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 6b</a>). A review of the literature on sunflower-oil synthesis showed that our network captured all 40 genes that have already been described (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 8</a>), demonstrating the sensitivity of the approach.</p><p>To find evidence of selection during sunflower breeding, we mapped resequencing data of 80 genotypes and measured differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>st</sub>) between oil and non-oil (for example, confectionary) types of domesticated lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 8.2</a>). Genes of the oil metabolic network were enriched in the top differentiated genes, suggesting that we had successfully identified relevant candidates for oil improvement. We found 46 oil genes in 32 genomic regions corresponding to previously identified QTLs for seven oil-related traits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 8.2</a>). Nine of these genes were highly differentiated between high- and low-oil lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 6c</a>), including <i>FAD2-1</i>, which has been shown to be under selection during post-domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Merah, O. et al. Genetic analysis of phytosterol content in sunflower seeds. Theor. Appl. Genet. 125, 1589–1601 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1890">24</a></sup>. Another, <i>HPPD</i>, had already been found to co-localize with a QTL for the vitamin E precursor tocopherol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Haddadi, P. et al. Genetic dissection of tocopherol and phytosterol in recombinant inbred lines of sunflower through quantitative trait locus analysis and the candidate gene approach. Mol. Breed. 29, 717–729 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1897">25</a></sup>. Our data suggest that this gene may have been targeted by selection. The remaining seven genes mainly mapped onto the diacylglycerol and linoleate biosynthesis pathways (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 6d, e</a>). In particular, a member of the PAP2 superfamily, which is involved in biosynthesis of fatty acid precursors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Carman, G. M. &amp; Han, G.-S. Roles of phosphatidate phosphatase enzymes in lipid metabolism. Trends Biochem. Sci. 31, 694–699 (2006)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1904">26</a></sup> and controls total lipid content in micro-algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Deng, X. D., Cai, J. J. &amp; Fei, X. W. Involvement of phosphatidate phosphatase in the biosynthesis of triacylglycerols in Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. J. Zhejiang Univ. Sci. B 14, 1121–1131 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1908">27</a></sup>, was predominantly expressed in seeds and co-localized with a QTL for total oil content. It therefore constitutes a strong candidate to improve this character (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 6f</a>).</p><p>The availability of this reference genome and companion resources will not only strengthen interest in the sunflower as a model for ecological and evolutionary studies, but will also accelerate breeding programs. In addition to the genome-wide association study of flowering time presented here, precisely mapping loci that contribute to other ecologically and agriculturally important traits in wild and domesticated individuals will enable precision breeding through marker-assisted and genomic selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bolger, M. E. et al. Plant genome sequencing — applications for crop improvement. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 26, 31–37 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1919">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kang, Y. J. et al. Translational genomics for plant breeding with the genome sequence explosion. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1057–1069 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1922">29</a></sup>. Functional validation of GWAS candidates will provide insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying variation in these traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Curtin, S. J. et al. Validating genome-wide association candidates controlling quantitative variation in nodulation. Plant Physiol. 173, 921–931 (2017)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1926">30</a></sup>. The sunflower now has the potential to become a model crop for climate change adaptation, which can be achieved by exploiting genome-enabled systems biology and multi-disciplinary analyses of interactions between abiotic stressors, pathogen attacks and agronomic practices.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>A full description of the Methods can be found in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>. No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The genome-wide association experiments were fully randomized and the investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly of the XRQ genotype</h3><p><i>Sequencing</i>. The DNA of the INRA inbred genotype XRQ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.1</a>) was extracted following a previously published protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Mayjonade, B. et al. Extraction of high-molecular-weight genomic DNA for long-read sequencing of single molecules. Biotechniques 61, 203–205 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1953">31</a></sup>, and sequenced using 407 SMRT cells with P6/C4 chemistry. Subreads were obtained using the SMRT Analysis RS.Subreads.1 pipeline (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.2</a>). In total 32.8 million subreads were generated with an <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> of 13.7 kb and a mean length of 10.3 kb. The targeted genome coverage of 102× was obtained with 367 Gb of raw sequence (340 Gb of subread data).</p><p><i>Assembly</i>. The PBcR wgs8.3rc1 assembly pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1969">32</a></sup> was used to perform the correction of reads, WGS 8.3 to assemble the corrected reads and quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d38746e1973">33</a></sup> to polish the consensus sequence after the construction of the pseudomolecules (see below). However, to overcome challenges associated with the sunflower genome assembly, substantial parameter tuning, code modification and software development were required and these are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.3–1.7</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Physical map construction, genetic map construction and assembly of pseudomolecules</h3><p>To develop a robust physical map for the sunflower that could be used to help to place sequence contigs on chromosomes and determine the physical length of gaps between them, bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) libraries were constructed for genotype HA412-HO by the French Plant Genome Resource Center (<a href="http://cnrgv.toulouse.inra.fr/en/library/sunflower">http://cnrgv.toulouse.inra.fr/en/library/sunflower</a>). We used 382,464 clones from the three BAC libraries to develop a 12.5× physical map, which was integrated with high-density genetic maps (see below). The resulting physical map covers approximately 3.3 Gb (around 92.5% of the 3.6 Gb genome) and is publicly available at <a href="https://www.sunflowergenome.org/">https://www.sunflowergenome.org/</a>.</p><p>We developed several high-density genetic maps that we used for correctly placing and ordering BAC and sequence contigs on chromosomes, as well as for the association and QTL analyses. While individual maps had gaps with no mappable markers owing to identity by descent, this problem was minimized by the use of multiple mapping populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.5</a>). The pseudomolecules were assembled as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.6</a>, leading to a final assembly of 17 pseudomolecules and 1,509 unanchored contigs. A web browser of this genome assembly is available at <a href="https://www.heliagene.org/HanXRQ-SUNRISE/">https://www.heliagene.org/HanXRQ-SUNRISE/</a>.</p><p>Sequencing, assembly and annotation of the genome of another genotype, HA412-HO, is presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.7</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation of protein-coding genes and lncRNAs</h3><p>Gene models were predicted using EuGene 4.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinform. 3, 87–97 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2031">34</a>) embedded in a new and fully automated pipeline that integrates probabilistic sequence model training, genome masking, transcript- and protein-alignment computation and alternative splice site detection. The plant early release of BUSCO (release July 2015)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2035">35</a></sup> was run on the set of predicted transcripts, and it detected 92% of complete gene models (590 complete single copy and 291 duplicated, respectively) plus 10 additional fragmented gene models.</p><p>Protein-coding genes were annotated using a three-step process, taking into account reciprocal best hits in the SwissProt and TAIR10 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis information resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1202–D1210 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2041">36</a>) databases (12,360 sunflower proteins), protein-domain content using Interpro (26,646 sunflower proteins), and similarity with plant proteomes (Ensembl release 30) or coverage of the transcript with RNA-sequencing data (1,200 predicted proteins with similarities in other plant proteomes without expression support, 1,832 with similarities in other plant proteomes with expression support and 8,542 gene models supported by expression data, but without significant hits with other plant proteomes). The remaining 1,663 predicted proteins remained completely uncharacterized. Details of the gene prediction and annotation process are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Annotation of small RNA</h3><p>To identify <i>H. annuus</i> miRNA genes, we constructed a small-RNA library using mixed RNAs from the various organs in control conditions (as for RNA sequencing) and sequenced them using Illumina GAIIx (oriented single-end 50 nucleotides (nt)). A total of 139 million reads were obtained that classically displayed a size distribution with two peaks of 21 and 24 nt small RNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.2</a>). Genome-wide prediction of miRNAs was performed combining Shortstack version 3.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Axtell, M. J. ShortStack: comprehensive annotation and quantification of small RNA genes. RNA 19, 740–751 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2062">37</a>) and an adapted version of the pipeline described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Formey, D. et al. The small RNA diversity from Medicago truncatula roots under biotic interactions evidences the environmental plasticity of the miRNAome. Genome Biol. 15, 457 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2065">38</a>, post-processed with the stringent criteria proposed by MiRBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kozomara, A. &amp; Griffiths-Jones, S. miRBase: annotating high confidence microRNAs using deep sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D68–D73 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2069">39</a></sup>. Targets of miRNA were predicted using miRanda version 3.0 (<a href="http://www.microrna.org">http://www.microrna.org</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Annotation of repeats</h3><p>LTR-RTs were annotated with an in-house pipeline that uses LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2089">40</a></sup> and LTRdigest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002–7013 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2093">41</a></sup>. DNA transposons were annotated with a custom pipeline that includes the ‘gt tirvish’ command, which is part of the GenomeTools suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gremme, G., Steinbiss, S. &amp; Kurtz, S. GenomeTools: a comprehensive software library for efficient processing of structured genome annotations. IEEE/ACM Trans. Comput. Biol. Bioinform. 10, 645–656 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2097">42</a></sup>. The age of LTR-RTs was determined by obtaining a likelihood divergence estimate between the LTRs with baseml from PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2101">43</a></sup> and using this divergence value (hereafter <i>d</i>) to calculate the LTR-RT age with the equation <i>T</i> = <i>d/2r</i>, where <i>r</i> = 1 × 10<sup>−8</sup> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Strasburg, J. L. &amp; Rieseberg, L. H. Molecular demographic history of the annual sunflowers Helianthus annuus and H. petiolaris—large effective population sizes and rates of long-term gene flow. Evolution 62, 1936–1950 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2119">44</a>). The total transposable element content was estimated to be 74.7 ± 0.08% (mean ± s.d.) on the basis of analyses with Transposome from random sequence reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2.3.3</a>). The detailed annotation pipeline of repeated elements is described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Sunflower palaeogenomics</h3><p>A comparative analysis was performed with sunflower, artichoke<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2138">16</a></sup>, coffee<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181–1184 (2014)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2142">17</a></sup> and lettuce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Truco, M. J. et al. An ultra-high-density, transcript-based, genetic map of lettuce. G3 (Bethesda) 3, 617–631 (2013)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2146">15</a></sup> and with grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2150">18</a></sup> as the outgroup. Identification of orthology and paralogy relationships, measurements of sequence divergence and estimation of divergence time through the level of synonymous substitutions were performed as detailed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 3.1</a> on the basis of the methods described in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salse, J., Abrouk, M., Murat, F., Quraishi, U. M. &amp; Feuillet, C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Brief. Bioinform. 10, 619–630 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2157">45</a> and the Timetree web service to estimate speciation dates (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>). Speciation events were dated to 38 million years ago (Ma) for sunflower–artichoke, 100 Ma for sunflower–coffee and 118 Ma for sunflower–grape. Palaeoploidization events were dated to 122–164 Ma for WGT-γ, 38–50 Ma for WGT-1 and 29 Ma for WGD-2.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Ancestry of the sunflower genome</h3><p>To identify introgressed regions in the XRQ and HA412-HO genome assemblies, we used previously published transcriptome sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Baute, G. J., Kane, N. C., Grassa, C. J., Lai, Z. &amp; Rieseberg, L. H. Genome scans reveal candidate domestication and improvement genes in cultivated sunflower, as well as post-domestication introgression with wild relatives. New Phytol. 206, 830–838 (2015)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2176">22</a></sup> from 60 genotypes representing native North-American landraces (that is, early domesticates), and several wild species that are probable donors to modern cultivated lines based on pedigree information, <i>H. argophyllus</i>, <i>H. petiolaris</i> and <i>H. tuberosus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3.2.1</a>). Raw reads were aligned to the genome assemblies and filtered as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 3.2</a>. To identify introgressed regions in the genomes of XRQ and HA412-HO we used the ‘site-by-site’ linkage admixture model in STRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Pritchard, J. K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2196">46</a></sup>(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 3.2</a>). Genome-wide and window estimates of introgression are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3.2.2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 3.2.1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">3.2.2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Transcriptome sequencing and analysis</h3><p>We generated 58 paired-end RNA-sequencing libraries to measure expression in 11 sunflower organs, the responses to hormonal and osmotic and salt treatments in roots and leaves, as well as response to variable water status (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4.1</a>). Library sequencing was done with Illumina HiSeq, reads were mapped with the glint software (<a href="https://forge-dga.jouy.inra.fr/projects/glint">https://forge-dga.jouy.inra.fr/projects/glint</a>) and only the best scoring pair(s) of reads was(were) kept. Expression measurements and normalization were performed as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4.2</a>. Organ-specificity was measured by computing a specificity index, Tau<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Yanai, I. et al. Genome-wide midrange transcription profiles reveal expression level relationships in human tissue specification. Bioinformatics 21, 650–659 (2005)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2233">47</a></sup>, on the normalized expression score. We identified sets of organ-specific genes and regulators (transcription factors and lncRNAs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4.2</a>). Analysis of differential expression in response to hormones and stress treatments were performed with the glm model of EdgeR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139–140 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2244">48</a></sup> as detailed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4.2</a>. Gene Ontology enrichment tests were carried out with Blast2GO Pro (one-sided Fisher’s exact tests, false discovery rate of &lt;0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Resequencing of domesticated lines</h3><p>We resequenced 80 lines of the sunflower mapping population (SAM) that represent the diversity of the cultivated sunflower. Statistics on resequenced lines are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5.1.1</a>. Seventy-two parent lines of the 480 hybrids used in a genome-wide association analysis of flowering time were also resequenced. The paired-end libraries were resequenced with Illumina HiSeq, read mapping was performed with the glint software (<a href="https://forge-dga.jouy.inra.fr/projects/glint">https://forge-dga.jouy.inra.fr/projects/glint</a>) and SNP calling with VarScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Koboldt, D. C. et al. VarScan: variant detection in massively parallel sequencing of individual and pooled samples. Bioinformatics 25, 2283–2285 (2009)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2269">49</a></sup>(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes 5.1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">5.2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Identification of flowering time orthologues and in-paralogues</h3><p>Flowering time genes in <i>A. thaliana</i> were retrieved from a recently developed database, FLOR-ID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bouché, F., Lobet, G., Tocquin, P. &amp; Périlleux, C. FLOR-ID: an interactive database of flowering-time gene networks in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nucleic Acids Res. 44 (D1), D1167–D1171 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2291">20</a></sup>, which includes 295 protein-coding genes and 11 miRNA genes and describes their interactions. We built gene clusters for a set of seven species, namely <i>H. annuus</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Cynara cardunculus</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Hordeum vulgare</i>, <i>Brassica rapa</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, chosen to be consistent with a previous study that identified orthologues for more than 30 flowering-time genes in the sunflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Blackman, B. K. et al. Contributions of flowering time genes to sunflower domestication and improvement. Genetics 187, 271–287 (2011)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2317">21</a></sup>, adding the proteome of the recently sequenced member of Asterids II <i>C. cardunculus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2324">16</a></sup>. To identify orthologues and in-paralogues (that is, paralogues post-dating speciation) of <i>A. thaliana</i> genes, we built and visually examined trees for the clusters defined above (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 6.2</a>) and manually screened BLAST reports on the sunflower genome browser. We identified 485 orthologues and in-paralogues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 7</a>). A genome-wide association study of flowering time was performed on a set of 480 hybrids obtained from 72 inbred genotypes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary note 6.1</a>), and colocalization of flowering-time orthologues with flowering time QTLs was assessed with bedtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2340">50</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Analysis of paralogues dating from the most recent whole-genome duplication (WGD-2)</h3><p>Correlation of expression between WGD-2 paralogues was assessed quantitatively by measuring the Pearson correlation coefficient and qualitatively by counting the number of pairs of paralogues that belong to the same co-expression modules based on a weighted gene co-expression network constructed with WGCNA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 7</a>). Significance was tested with 1,000 permutations of the genes in the expression matrix. The level of functional diploidy of the genome for flowering time was measured as the number of pairs of WGD-2 paralogous genes or paralogous genomic regions for which both members of the pair (that is, both paralogous genes or both paralogous genomic regions) intersected with genomic intervals corresponding to flowering-time QTLs. Paralogous blocks were identified by a chaining approach detailed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 7</a>. Observed counts were compared to a null distribution obtained from 1,000 permutations of flowering-time QTLs for several sets of parameters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#Tab1">Extended Data Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Reconstruction of oil metabolic pathways</h3><p>The metabolic annotation of protein sequences was performed with the E2P2 software (version 3.0, <a href="https://dpb.carnegiescience.edu/labs/rhee-lab/software">https://dpb.carnegiescience.edu/labs/rhee-lab/software</a>). We used the pathway-tools software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Karp, P. D., Paley, S. &amp; Romero, P. The pathway tools software. Bioinformatics 18 (Suppl 1), S225–S232 (2002)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2379">51</a></sup> to infer biochemical reactions and metabolic pathways from the protein annotations. The super pathway of sunflower oil metabolism was created on the basis of the main components of the known sunflower oil metabolism by merging 16 pathways, and it includes 125 reactions, 160 metabolites and 429 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 8.1</a>). Web resources for exploring the sunflower metabolism network are available at <a href="https://www.heliagene.org/HanXRQ-SUNRISE/data/analyses/metabolism">https://www.heliagene.org/HanXRQ-SUNRISE/data/analyses/metabolism</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Integrative candidate genes analysis for oil metabolism</h3><p>We measured the <i>F</i><sub>st</sub> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Hudson, R. R., Slatkin, M. &amp; Maddison, W. P. Estimation of levels of gene flow from DNA sequence data. Genetics 132, 583–589 (1992)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2404">52</a>) between lines cultivated for oil production and other lines (mainly confectionary for human consumption) with egglib version 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="De Mita, S. &amp; Siol, M. EggLib: processing, analysis and simulation tools for population genetics and genomics. BMC Genet. 13, 27 (2012)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2407">53</a>). Genes of the oil super pathway that possessed an <i>F</i><sub>st</sub> score above the 95th percentile were further examined. Forty-nine previously published QTLs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Ebrahimi, A. et al. QTL mapping of seed-quality traits in sunflower recombinant inbred lines under different water regimes. Genome 51, 599–615 (2008)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2416">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pérez-Vich, B. et al. Molecular basis of the high-palmitic acid trait in sunflower seed oil. Mol. Breed. 36, 43 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2419">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Premnath, A., Narayana, M., Ramakrishnan, C., Kuppusamy, S. &amp; Chockalingam, V. Mapping quantitative trait loci controlling oil content, oleic acid and linoleic acid content in sunflower (Helianthus annuus L.). Mol. Breed. 36, 106 (2016)" href="/articles/nature22380#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d38746e2422">56</a></sup> were mapped to the XRQ genome assembly and 5 Mb were added at the flanks of the mapped markers to define the QTL coordinates and assess colocalization with candidate genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature22380#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 8.2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Data availability</h3><p>This whole genome shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession <a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/search?query=MNCJ00000000">MNCJ00000000</a>. Transcriptome and resequencing sequence reads have been deposited in the SRA database as studies <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP092899">SRP092899</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP092742">SRP092742</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP093222">SRP093222</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP095974">SRP095974</a>.</p></div></div></section>

M.C., B.Man., J.M.B. and J.S. contributed equally to this work. A.Bel., H.Be. and N.H. prepared BAC libraries. B.May. developed the DNA-extraction protocol for PacBio sequencing. B.May., C.V. and C.D. performed PacBio sequencing. A.Bér., D.B., D.V., E.Ma., E.B.-M., G.Marc., G.Mara., J.R.M., J.T., L.Lo., M.-C.B., M.-C.L.P., N.B., N.B.L., N.P., S.N., S.V., Y.L. and Z.L. contributed to DNA/RNA sample collection and data production. O.C. performed flow cytometry experiments. N.G., T.N. and N.C.K. built the physical map and integrated the physical and genetic maps. C.J.G., S.M., J.E.B. and J.M.B. developed genetic maps. J.G. assembled the XRQ genome. C.J.G. built the XRQ pseudomolecules. C.C. performed quality control of XRQ pseudomolecules. C.J.G., J.E.B., N.C.K., S.H. and M.K. assembled the HA412-HO genome. J.G., E.S. and T.S. annotated protein-coding genes and miRNA (XRQ). S.E.S. annotated the HA412-HO genome. S.C., J.G., F.R., M.K., T.F., C.J.G., J.E.B., N.C.K., N.G., T.N., N.C., E.Mo. developed bioinformatics resources. L.Le., E.Ma. and G.F. performed bioinformatics analyses. G.L.O. conducted ancestry analyses. P.V. designed the GWAS hybrid panel. B.Man., N.B.L. and P.V. designed the GWAS experiment. F.V. developed the XRQ inbred line. B.Man., P.P.-E. conducted the GWAS analysis. L.C. conducted metabolism analyses. F.M. and J.S. conducted palaeo-evolution analyses. S.E.S. conducted repeat analyses. C.L.-B. and M.C. conducted small-RNA analyses. H.Ba., S.M. and N.B.L. performed integrated analyses on flowering time and oil metabolism. H.Ba. and N.B.L. performed transcriptomic analysis. H.Ba. performed analysis of sunflower ohnologues. S.J.K. contributed to the genome consortium coordination. N.B.L., L.H.R., P.V., S.M., J.M.B. and J.G. designed experiments and coordinated the project. L.H.R. coordinated the sunflower genome consortium. H.Ba., N.B.L. and L.H.R. wrote the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
